Per the FoxIO spec the QUIC JA4 is **identical to the TLS-over-TCP JA4 bar the leading protocol character** (`q` not `t`), so `internal/tlsdecode`'s `computeJA4` / `computeJA4S` were parameterised on the protocol prefix (the existing TLS path still passes `t`, anchors unchanged) and a new exported `tlsdecode.QUICHandshakeJA4` parses the bare CRYPTO-stream handshake message (no TLS record envelope, RFC 9001 §4.1) and computes the `q` fingerprint, which `internal/quic` calls only for a complete contiguous handshake (a partial ClientHello would fingerprint wrong → surface nothing, no confidently-wrong output). **Verified end-to-end against FoxIO's own QUIC pcap**: extracted the QUIC Initial from `pcap/quic-tls-handshake.pcapng`, decrypted it with our v0.526 code, reassembled the Chrome ClientHello, and reproduced FoxIO's published snapshot JA4 `q13d0310h3_55b375c5d22e_cd85d2d88918` byte-for-byte (the full pcap→decrypt→ClientHello→JA4 chain, the real-world dog-food of the decryptor); the JA4S `q` variant is anchored to the same snapshot's `q130200_1301_234ea6891581` (the `q`-prefix of the already-verified TCP `t130200_1301_234ea6891581`). Native — extends the existing JA4 machinery + the v0.526 decryptor, stdlib + already-present `golang.org/x/crypto/hkdf`, no new dep. No new tool — extends `quic_long_header_decode` (`internal/quic` → `internal/tlsdecode`); registry unchanged. The JA4+ family now spans every layer **and** both transports: TCP (JA4T) / TLS (JA4/JA4S) / HTTP (JA4H) / cert (JA4X) over TCP, plus JA4/JA4S over QUIC.
- `quic_long_header_decode` Initial decryption — ✅ v0.526.0 — **decrypts the QUIC v1 Initial packet** — the one long-header packet whose protection keys are *public* (HKDF-derived from the clear-text Destination Connection ID + a fixed per-version salt, RFC 9001 §5.2, with no TLS-handshake secret), so it is fully decryptable offline from the packet bytes alone. The package previously surfaced the Initial payload as protected hex and explicitly deferred decryption; now `internal/quic` derives the client/server Initial keys (HKDF-Extract + HKDF-Expand-Label), removes header protection (AES-128-ECB over the 16-byte sample → mask → unprotect the first byte's low nibble + the 1-4 packet-number bytes), runs AES-128-GCM (nonce = iv XOR padded packet number, AAD = the unprotected header), then dissects the cleartext frames (PADDING run-collapsed / PING / ACK+ACK-ECN parsed-to-skip / CRYPTO / CONNECTION_CLOSE) and **reassembles the CRYPTO stream into the TLS ClientHello / ServerHello** — the bytes QUIC otherwise hides, ready to paste into `tls_handshake_decode` for the full JA4 / ALPN / SNI view. `Decode` tries the client role then the server role automatically. **Verified byte-for-byte against the RFC 9001 Appendix A.2 worked example**: the full 1200-byte protected client Initial (DCID `8394c8f03e515708`) reproduces every documented intermediate — key `1f369613…`, iv `fa044b2f…`, hp `9f50449e…`, sample `d1b1c98d…`, mask `437b9aec36`, unprotected first byte `c3`, AAD `c300…0002`, nonce `fa04…255e` — and decrypts to the documented CRYPTO frame (offset 0, length 241) carrying the ClientHello, followed by 917 padding-zero bytes. Wrong-role decryption fails GCM authentication (integrity holds). Only QUIC v1 is decrypted — v2 / drafts use a different salt + `quicv2 ` key labels with no published vector to anchor, so they're held rather than risk a wrong decode. Native — stdlib AES/GCM + `golang.org/x/crypto/hkdf` (already a dependency); no new runtime dep, no shell-out. Fuzz-clean (4.4M execs, no panic). No new tool — extends `quic_long_header_decode` (`internal/quic`); registry unchanged.
- `geneve_decode` inner-IP decode — ✅ v0.479.0 — **completes the tunnel-decap sweep** (GRE → GTP-U → VXLAN → Geneve). Geneve (RFC 8926; VMware NSX-T / OVN / Antrea overlays, UDP 6081) is the richest case: its protocol-type field gives both forms — 0x6558 (Transparent Ethernet Bridging) wraps an inner Ethernet frame (decoded like VXLAN: when its EtherType is IP, the L3 packet is decoded via `internal/ipdecode`), while 0x0800 / 0x86DD wrap an IP packet directly (decoded like GRE). Either way the encapsulated flow's addresses / protocol / ports surface in place. Non-IP forms (MPLS / NSH, or non-IP inner EtherTypes like 802.1Q) are left as hex; an IP-typed payload that fails to parse → `inner_decode_error` + raw hex. Verified with direct-IPv4/IPv6 + TEB-inner-IPv4 + non-IP + garbage cases; added a geneve fuzz harness, fuzz-clean through both inner→ipdecode paths. No new tool — extends `geneve_decode` (`internal/geneve` → `internal/ipdecode`). **Tunnel-decap family now complete.**
- `vxlan_decode` inner-IP decode — ✅ v0.478.0 — third of the tunnel-decap sweep, and the first **inner-Ethernet** case: a VXLAN packet (overlay networks / cloud / Kubernetes CNI / NSX, UDP 4789) encapsulates an Ethernet frame, and when its EtherType is IPv4 (0x0800) / IPv6 (0x86DD) the inner L3 packet is now **decoded in place** via `internal/ipdecode` (the decoder already parsed the inner Ethernet header — dst/src MAC + EtherType — so the L3 bytes start at the known payload offset), surfacing the overlaid flow's addresses / protocol / ports. Non-IP inner frames (ARP, 802.1Q, MPLS, …) are left for their own decoders; an IP-typed inner frame that fails to parse is reported with `inner_decode_error` + raw hex preserved. Verified with inner IPv4/UDP + IPv6/UDP + ARP-not-decoded + garbage cases; added a vxlan fuzz harness, fuzz-clean through the inner→ipdecode path. No new tool — extends `vxlan_decode` (`internal/vxlan` → `internal/ipdecode`). (Geneve is the last sibling; its inner is also Ethernet.)
- `gtp_decode` inner-IP decode — ✅ v0.477.0 — second of the tunnel-decap sweep: a GTP-U **G-PDU** (message type 0xFF — the 4G/5G mobile-core user-plane tunnel) carries the subscriber's IP packet directly, now **decoded in place** via `internal/ipdecode` (the version nibble is self-describing — GTP-U has no protocol-type field), surfacing the tunnelled flow's addresses / protocol / ports. Gated on message type 0xFF (Echo / Error-Indication / End-Marker etc. carry no IP payload and are left alone); a G-PDU payload that fails to parse as IP is reported with `inner_decode_error` + raw hex preserved. Verified with inner IPv4/UDP + IPv6/UDP vectors + non-G-PDU + garbage cases; fuzz-clean through the inner→ipdecode path (existing gtp harness). No new tool — extends `gtp_decode` (`internal/gtp` → `internal/ipdecode`). (Geneve / VXLAN remain — their inner is Ethernet, needing an L2 strip first.)
- `gre_decode` inner-IP decode — ✅ v0.476.0 — extended the chain-to-existing-decoder seam to **tunnel decapsulation**: a GRE packet (PPTP / VPN / MPLS-over-GRE — IP protocol 47) encapsulates an inner packet, and when the GRE protocol-type field marks it IPv4 (0x0800) or IPv6 (0x86DD) the inner packet is now **decoded in place** via `internal/ipdecode`, surfacing the tunnelled flow's source/dest addresses, protocol and ports directly in the GRE decode. Non-IP payload kinds (Transparent Ethernet 0x6558, ARP, MPLS, …) are left as hex (no confidently-wrong output), and an IP-typed payload that fails to parse is reported with `inner_decode_error` + raw hex preserved. Verified with inner IPv4/UDP + IPv6/UDP vectors (inner addresses + ports asserted) + non-IP-not-decoded + IP-typed-garbage cases; added a gre fuzz harness, fuzz-clean through the inner→ipdecode path. The first of the tunnel family (GTP-U / Geneve / VXLAN are the natural follow-on sweep). No new tool — extends `gre_decode` (`internal/gre` → `internal/ipdecode`).
- `icmp_packet_decode` embedded-packet decode — ✅ v0.475.0 — continued the chain-to-existing-decoder seam: an ICMP **error** message (Destination Unreachable / Time Exceeded / Parameter Problem / Redirect / Packet-Too-Big) quotes the original IP packet that triggered it (the IP header + ≥8 bytes of payload), and that quote was previously surfaced as raw hex "to feed into `ip_packet_decode`". It is now **decoded in place** via `internal/ipdecode`, so the offending flow's source/dest addresses, protocol and (for UDP, or a long-enough TCP quote) ports appear directly in the ICMP decode. Also added v6 Destination Unreachable (type 1) / Time Exceeded (type 3) — previously raw-hex — routing their embedded IPv6 packet through the same path. No confidently-wrong output: a quote that doesn't parse as IP is reported with `embedded_decode_error` and the raw hex preserved (truncated TCP quotes degrade gracefully — IP layer + ports as available). Verified with embedded IPv4/UDP + IPv6/UDP vectors (inner addresses + ports asserted) + a garbage-quote fallback; added an icmp fuzz harness, fuzz-clean through the embedded→ipdecode path. No new tool — extends `icmp_packet_decode` (`internal/icmp` → `internal/ipdecode`).
- `dtls_decode` Certificate body — ✅ v0.474.0 — sibling-swept the v0.473 TLS Certificate decode into **DTLS**: the DTLS Certificate handshake message (msg_type 11) body uses the same TLS 1.2 layout, so each DER cert is chained through `internal/x509decode` (subject / issuer / validity / SAN / fingerprints / CA). Gated on the DTLS handshake message being **unfragmented** (the existing dispatch already returns early on a fragment, so a partial cert is never mis-decoded); mismatched list-length → note; unparseable DER → raw + `decode_error`. Self-verified with `crypto/x509`-generated certs (single + chain) wrapped in a DTLS 1.2 Certificate record; added a DTLS fuzz harness (cert→x509 seed), fuzz-clean. No new tool — extends `dtls_decode` (`internal/dtls` → `internal/x509decode`).
- `ip_packet_decode` JA4T fingerprint — ✅ v0.525.0 — added the **JA4T TCP-SYN fingerprint** (FoxIO) to `ip_packet_decode` — a **passive OS / TCP-stack fingerprint**, the modern p0f analogue and a genuinely distinct capability class from the TLS/HTTP/cert JA4 members. From a SYN it emits `window_size_option-kinds_MSS_window-scale` (e.g. `65535_2-1-3-1-1-8-4-0-0_1460_6`): the TCP window size, the exact wire sequence of TCP option *kinds* (every NOP and EOL/padding byte included, in order), the MSS, and the window scale. **No hashes — pure structural extraction**, so there's no confidently-wrong hash risk; the only subtlety was that the display option-list stops at the first EOL while JA4T counts every trailing padding byte, so JA4T gets its own option walk. Computed only for SYN packets (the options are negotiated at setup). **Verified byte-for-byte against the FoxIO `macos_tcp_flags` snapshot** by extracting the actual SYN's IP packet from FoxIO's pcap and confirming the parse reproduces `65535_2-1-3-1-1-8-4-0-0_1460_6` exactly (both trailing zeros included). Native — extends the existing TCP-header+options decode, stdlib only, no new dep. No new tool — extends `ip_packet_decode` (`internal/ipdecode`); registry unchanged. The full JA4+ family is now JA4 / JA4S / JA4X / JA4H / JA4T — five members, all byte-for-byte verified, fingerprinting the TCP, TLS, HTTP, and certificate layers.
- `http_message_decode` JA4H fingerprint — ✅ v0.524.0 — added the **JA4H HTTP-client fingerprint** (FoxIO), **completing the entire JA4+ family**: JA4 (client TLS, v0.521) + JA4S (server TLS, v0.522) + JA4X (cert, v0.523) + JA4H (HTTP, this) now fingerprint every layer a connection touches. JA4H identifies the HTTP client stack (browser / library / bot / malware) and is computed for requests in `internal/httpmsg`: `method + version + cookie/referer flags + header-count + Accept-Language` `_` `hash12(header names)` `_` `hash12(cookie names)` `_` `hash12(cookie name=value)` — header names hashed in **wire case + order** (a client signal, not lower-cased), Cookie/Referer/pseudo-headers excluded from the count and the header hash, cookies sorted by name. JA4H had been **HELD three fires running** as "needs tshark" — this fire I cracked it by reading the full `ja4h.py` reference algorithm, then **extracting the actual HTTP requests as plaintext directly from FoxIO's own test pcaps** (`http-empty-useragent.pcap`, `http1-with-cookies.pcapng`) and anchoring byte-for-byte against the snapshot outputs: a 1-header GET → `ge10nn010000_b8bcd45ac095_…` (which revealed the rule I'd been getting wrong — header names are hashed in **original case**: `sha256("User-Agent")` = `b8bcd45ac095`, not `sha256("user-agent")`), and a curl GET with Referer + 2 cookies + `Accept-Language: da` → `ge11cr04da00_8ddaef5d77af_280f366eaa04_c2fb0fe53442` (all three sub-hashes reproduced exactly, anchoring the cookie-name-sort + name=value + language transform). Native — `crypto/sha256` + field assembly over the already-parsed request, stdlib only, no new dep. No new tool — extends `http_message_decode` (`internal/httpmsg`); registry unchanged. **The JA4+ family is now complete and every member is byte-for-byte verified.**
- `x509_certificate_decode` JA4X fingerprint — ✅ v0.523.0 — added the **JA4X X.509-certificate fingerprint** (FoxIO), completing the **verifiable JA4+ family** alongside JA4 (client, v0.521) and JA4S (server, v0.522): JA4 → JA4S → JA4X fingerprint the client stack, the server stack, and the **certificate-generation stack** respectively — and malware C2 / phishing infrastructure reuses cert-generation patterns across deployments, making JA4X a real threat-intel pivot. JA4X = `hash12(issuer RDN OIDs)_hash12(subject RDN OIDs)_hash12(extension OIDs)`, each the comma-joined hex of the OID DER content-octets **in certificate order** (not sorted). Computed in `internal/x509decode` from the already-parsed cert, so it surfaces in both `x509_certificate_decode` and every cert in a `tls_handshake_decode` chain. **Verified by the same reverse-engineering approach that cracked JA4S**: three recurring RDN hashes from the FoxIO browsers-x509 snapshot were reproduced byte-for-byte — `hash12("550406,55040a,550403")` = `a373a9f83c6b` (C,O,CN), C,O,OU,CN = `7d5dbb3783b4`, C,ST,L,O,CN = `2bab15409345` — plus an end-to-end anchor (a generated C,O,CN self-signed cert with SKI/AKI/BasicConstraints decodes to `a373a9f83c6b_a373a9f83c6b_795797892f9c`). Cross-confirmed against the FoxIO rust reference (`ja4x/src/lib.rs`: OIDs as `hex(attr_type().as_bytes())`, joined, not sorted). Native — `crypto/x509` ordered RDN/extension OIDs + `encoding/asn1` OID marshaling + SHA-256, stdlib only, no new dep. No new tool — extends `x509_certificate_decode` (`internal/x509decode`); registry unchanged. Only JA4H (HTTP) now remains deferred (no inline vectors; needs tshark).
- `tls_handshake_decode` JA4S fingerprint — ✅ v0.522.0 — added the **JA4S TLS *server* fingerprint** (FoxIO), the server-side counterpart to v0.521's JA4 client fingerprint — together they fingerprint **both ends** of a TLS session (client-stack + server-stack / C2 identification). Computed from the ServerHello (which `tls_handshake_decode` already decodes): JA4S = `protocol(t) + TLS-version + 2-digit server-extension-count + chosen-ALPN` `_` `negotiated-cipher(4-hex)` `_` `SHA256(server extensions in WIRE ORDER)[:12]`. **The crucial difference from JA4: the server extensions are NOT sorted** — the server's extension order is itself the fingerprint signal. I'd HELD on JA4S two fires earlier (FoxIO publishes no inline worked example), then **cracked the verification by reverse-engineering it against two real FoxIO snapshot outputs**: the same two TLS-1.3 ServerHello extensions (key_share 0x0033 + supported_versions 0x002b) appear in **opposite order** for two server stacks — `SHA256("0033,002b")[:12]` = `234ea6891581` (Google → `t130200_1301_234ea6891581`) and `SHA256("002b,0033")[:12]` = `a56c5b993250` (LastPass → `t130200_1302_a56c5b993250`) — which both reproduce exactly *and* prove the wire-order rule. Cross-confirmed against the FoxIO rust reference source (`tls.rs`: "preserving the original order of server's TLS extensions"). An end-to-end ServerHello decode caught a real bug — the server's `supported_versions` extension is a bare 2-byte version (not the client's length-prefixed list), so the JA4S version is now driven from the parsed negotiated version. Native — SHA-256 + field assembly over the already-parsed ServerHello, stdlib only, no new dep. No new tool — extends `tls_handshake_decode` (`internal/tlsdecode`); registry unchanged. JA4H / JA4X remain deferred (no inline vectors; need tshark).
- `tls_handshake_decode` JA4 fingerprint — ✅ v0.521.0 — added the **JA4 TLS client fingerprint** (FoxIO LLC), the modern successor to the JA3 the decoder already computed and increasingly the preferred client / C2 / malware fingerprint for threat-intel (JA3 is being retired in its favour). It was explicitly listed as deferred in the package doc; now implemented from the already-parsed ClientHello. JA4 = `protocol + TLS-version + SNI-flag + non-GREASE-cipher-count + non-GREASE-extension-count + first-ALPN` `_` `SHA256(sorted ciphers)[:12]` `_` `SHA256(sorted extensions, SNI+ALPN removed, + signature_algorithms in order)[:12]`, with GREASE ignored throughout. **Anchored byte-for-byte to the FoxIO worked example** `t13d1516h2_8daaf6152771_e5627efa2ab1` — both truncated SHA-256 hashes reproduce the spec's exact pre-hash strings, the GREASE-exclusion is unit-tested, and an end-to-end real-ClientHello decode yields a well-formed `t13d…h2_…_…`. Raw `signature_algorithms` and `supported_versions` code points are now captured (alongside their existing name forms) to feed JA4_c and the JA4 version. Only the TLS-over-TCP client JA4 is computed; JA4S/JA4H/JA4X and the QUIC/DTLS variants remain deferred. No new tool — extends `tls_handshake_decode` (`internal/tlsdecode`); registry unchanged.
- `tls_handshake_decode` Certificate body — ✅ v0.473.0 decoded the TLS **Certificate handshake message** (previously labelled-but-deferred): the TLS 1.2 (and earlier) certificate_list is walked and each DER certificate is chained through `internal/x509decode`, so a captured handshake now yields the server's full cert chain — subject / issuer / validity / SAN / fingerprints / CA flag — in one decode (the network-pentest analogue of the NDEF→WSC/BT-OOB carrier wirings). In TLS 1.3 the Certificate message is encrypted (post-key-exchange), so the plaintext form in a passive capture is the pre-1.3 layout decoded here; a body that doesn't match it is reported with a note, never mis-parsed; a cert whose DER fails to parse is surfaced raw with a `decode_error` rather than a confident decode. Self-verified by wrapping `crypto/x509`-generated certs (single + chain) in a Certificate record and asserting the chained x509 fields, plus malformed-list-length / garbage-DER cases; fuzz-clean (5M execs through the cert→x509 path, 0 panics). No new tool — extends `tls_handshake_decode` (`internal/tlsdecode` → `internal/x509decode`).
- `epc_decode` SGTIN-198 — ✅ v0.472.0 extended `epc_decode` with **SGTIN-198** (header 0x36), the 198-bit SGTIN whose serial is a variable-length **alphanumeric** 7-bit-ASCII string (up to 20 chars) rather than a numeric value — used where the GS1 serial isn't purely numeric (e.g. pharmaceutical serialisation), and previously reported "unsupported". `Decode` now dispatches by length: 12 bytes → the 96-bit schemes, 25/26 bytes → SGTIN-198 (the word-aligned form a RAIN reader emits; the 140-bit serial is twenty null-padded 7-bit chars). Company prefix / item reference / GTIN-14 reconstruction are shared with SGTIN-96; only the serial decode differs. Anchored byte-for-byte against the epc-encoding-utils oracle — `3614257BF7194E60C286C5933…` → `sgtin-198:0.0614141.812345.ABC123` and `…6C59B4B5C8…` → `…812345.XYZ-9`. The other 198-bit variants (GRAI-170 / GIAI-202 / SGLN-195) remain reported-unsupported. No new tool. Fuzz-clean. (`internal/epc`)
- `epc_decode` SGLN-96 / GRAI-96 / GIAI-96 — ✅ v0.471.0 **completed the 96-bit EPC family** in `epc_decode` by adding the three GS1-keyed schemes deferred at v0.470 (the prior fire HELD on these for lack of an authoritative source): **SGLN-96** (header 0x32, location — company prefix + location reference + extension), **GRAI-96** (0x33, returnable asset — company prefix + asset type + serial), and **GIAI-96** (0x34, individual asset — company prefix + asset reference). The remaining-source problem was solved with the **venv-oracle technique**: the `epc-encoding-utils` Python library was installed and used as an oracle to extract the authoritative partition tables and generate decoded vectors, anchoring each scheme byte-for-byte — `3214257BF460720000000190` → `sgln-96:0.0614141.12345.400`, `3314257BF40C0E400000162E` → `grai-96:0.0614141.12345.5678`, `3414257BF400000000003039` → `giai-96:0.0614141.12345` (+ a 12-digit-prefix GIAI partition-0 vector). Each decodes filter / partition / company prefix / reference fields + the EPC tag and pure-identity URIs, matching the oracle's formatting exactly (per-scheme zero-padding rules). `epc_decode` now covers all six 96-bit schemes (SGTIN/SSCC/SGLN/GRAI/GIAI/GID); 198-bit variants remain reported-unsupported. No new tool. Fuzz-clean. (`internal/epc`)
- `epc_decode` GID-96 — ✅ v0.470.0 extended `epc_decode` with **GID-96** (EPC header 0x35), the General Identifier — the EPC scheme used when no GS1 key (GTIN/SSCC/GLN/…) applies. Three fixed-width fields, no partition table / company prefix / check digit: general manager number (28 bits) + object class (24) + serial number (36), plus the EPC tag / pure-identity URIs. **Verified byte-for-byte against three worked vectors** — `3500079FF00000B00000000C` → `gid-96:31231.11.12`, `3500E82D900000F000000001` → `gid-96:951001.15.1`, `350000A2600019003ADE56FA` → `gid-96:2598.400.987649786`. Remaining GS1-keyed schemes (SGLN/GRAI/GIAI, which still need their own partition tables) stay identified-not-decoded. No new tool. Fuzz-clean. (`internal/epc`)
- `epc_decode` SSCC-96 — ✅ v0.469.0 extended `epc_decode` with **SSCC-96** (EPC header 0x31), the GS1 Serial Shipping Container Code — the logistics-unit (case/pallet/container) scheme, the second-most-common EPC after SGTIN. Decodes filter / partition / company prefix / serial reference and reconstructs the **SSCC-18** (extension digit + company prefix + serial-reference remainder + recomputed GS1 mod-10 check digit) plus the EPC tag / pure-identity URIs. The SSCC partition table (distinct from SGTIN's — no item reference, wider serial reference) and the SSCC-18 reconstruction are **verified byte-for-byte against the worked vector** `3134257BF4499602D2000000` → `urn:epc:tag:sscc-96:1.0614141.1234567890` (company prefix 0614141, serial reference 1234567890, SSCC-18 106141412345678908). The 24 trailing reserved bits are checked (non-zero noted). Remaining 96-bit schemes (SGLN/GRAI/GIAI/GID) still identified-not-decoded. No new tool. Fuzz-clean. (`internal/epc`)
- `epc_decode` (GS1 EPC UHF RAIN RFID — SGTIN-96) — ✅ shipped v0.468.0 — decodes the GS1 **Electronic Product Code** binary on UHF RAIN RFID (EPC Gen2 / ISO 18000-63) tags — retail item-level tagging and supply-chain logistics — a **whole RFID band the toolkit did not decode** (it covered HF ISO 14443/15693/NDEF and LF EM4100/HID/FDX-B/T5577, but not UHF EPC). Reading a UHF tag needs a RAIN reader (hardware, out of scope), but the captured 96-bit EPC decodes entirely offline. **SGTIN-96** (header 0x30, the dominant retail scheme) is fully decoded: filter, partition, company prefix, item reference, serial number, the canonical EPC tag URI (`urn:epc:tag:sgtin-96:…`) and pure-identity URI (`urn:epc:id:sgtin:…`), and the reconstructed **GTIN-14** (with a recomputed GS1 mod-10 check digit). The SGTIN-96 layout, the partition table, and the SGTIN→GTIN reconstruction are taken from the **GS1 EPC Tag Data Standard** and **verified byte-for-byte against its canonical worked example** — `3074257BF7194E4000001A85` → `urn:epc:tag:sgtin-96:3.0614141.812345.6789` (company prefix 0614141, item ref 812345, serial 6789, GTIN-14 80614141123458) — not recalled. No confidently-wrong output: the other 96-bit schemes (SSCC/SGLN/GRAI/GIAI/GID, 0x31-0x35) are identified by name but not field-decoded (raw + note) rather than guessed; unknown headers and 198-bit variants are reported unsupported. Verified against the canonical vector + a second GS1 check-digit cross-check + other-scheme/unknown/error cases; fuzz-clean (3.4M execs, 0 panics). Native — fixed bit-field extraction + GS1 mod-10, stdlib only (`internal/epc`).
- `nfc_t2t_tlv_decode` (NFC Type 2 Tag data-area TLV walker) — ✅ shipped v0.467.0 — walks the TLV blocks in a Type 2 Tag's **data area** (user memory from page 4 onward) and **decodes the NDEF message in place**, bridging a raw tag-memory dump to `ndef_decode`: a Type 2 Tag (NTAG / MIFARE Ultralight) stores its NDEF inside a TLV structure (NULL 0x00 / Lock Control 0x01 / Memory Control 0x02 / NDEF 0x03 / Proprietary 0xFD / Terminator 0xFE), and this locates and decodes it. The data-area complement to `nfc_t2t` (which decodes the page 0-3 header: UID/BCC/lock/CC) — previously an operator had to manually extract the NDEF bytes from a dump to feed `ndef_decode`. Each block is reported with type/offset/length/raw value; the NDEF TLV value is handed to the shared NDEF walker (URI/Text/Smart Poster/handover/MIME). The 1-or-3-byte length form (0xFF big-endian escape) is handled and the Terminator ends the walk. The TLV type values + length encoding are from the **NFC Forum Type 2 Tag Operation Specification** (cross-checked vs the Nordic nrfxlib T2T docs). No confidently-wrong output: a TLV whose declared length overruns the buffer is reported as truncated, and the Lock/Memory Control reserved-area descriptors are surfaced raw rather than guessed. Verified against a full Lock+NDEF+Terminator data area (NDEF Text record decoded in place) + NULL-skip / 3-byte-length / unknown-type / truncated / no-terminator / error cases; fuzz-clean (3.8M execs, 0 panics). Native — TLV framing over the shared NDEF walker (`internal/t2t`).
- `ntag_config_decode` (NTAG213/215/216 configuration-page decode) — ✅ shipped v0.466.0 — decodes the NTAG21x **configuration pages**, the registers controlling an NFC Type-2 tag's password protection, lock state, NFC counter, and UID/counter ASCII-mirror — answering an operator's first questions about a dumped tag: is it password-protected, **read+write or write-only (PROT)**, is the config **permanently locked (CFGLCK)**, how many failed password attempts are allowed (**AUTHLIM**), is the NFC read counter enabled. The protection-state complement to `ndef_decode` / `nfc_t2t` (which decode the tag's *data*, not its access registers). Decodes **AUTH0** (first protected page), the **ACCESS** byte (PROT/CFGLCK/NFC_CNT_EN/NFC_CNT_PWD_PROT/AUTHLIM), and the **MIRROR** byte (MIRROR_CONF UID/counter mirror, MIRROR_BYTE/PAGE, strong-modulation) from CFG0+CFG1 (8 bytes; 16 bytes adds PWD+PACK). The config pages live at different addresses per variant (29h/83h/E3h) but the layout is identical, so it decodes the page bytes regardless of origin. The page layout (Table 8), MIRROR byte (Table 9), ACCESS byte (Table 10), and every field value meaning (Table 11) were **extracted and verified byte-for-byte from the NXP NTAG213/215/216 data sheet rev 3.2 §8.5.7** (read directly from the PDF — not recalled). No confidently-wrong output: an undocumented MIRROR_CONF value (0b11) is surfaced as reserved; AUTH0=0xFF (protection disabled) is noted rather than asserted against an unknown memory size. Verified against factory-default / read+write-protected / CFGLCK+mirror / NFC-counter / PWD+PACK / error cases; fuzz-clean (4.8M execs, 0 panics). Native — fixed bit-field extraction, stdlib only (`internal/ntag`).
- `t5577_config_encode` (T5577 / ATA5577 configuration-word build) — ✅ shipped v0.623.0 — the inverse of `t5577_config_decode` (v0.465): builds the 32-bit T5577 block-0 config word from its raw fields (modulation / data bit rate / max block + optional master key / PSK clock / AOR / PWD / ST). **Completes the offline clone-prep chain**: read a credential → generate the clone data block (the LF `*_encode` tools) → generate the config word that sets a T5577 blank up for the right protocol before the data blocks are written. **No confidently-wrong output**: the bit layout is the same Proxmark3-verified one `t5577_config_decode` uses, and the encoder **round-trips** with it (decoding the emitted word reproduces the fields) and reproduces the two reference configs (EM4100 → `00148040`, HID Prox → `00107060`); the PSK clock bits are only written for the PSK modulations (1-3), matching the decoder; out-of-range fields are rejected. Generation only — transmits nothing and writes to no device, so it stays Low like the decoder. Verified by the two reference-config reproductions and a decode∘encode round-trip across modulation/flag combinations; native — fixed bit-field placement, stdlib only, no new go.mod dep (`internal/t55xx`). Registry 614→615.
- `t5577_config_decode` (T5577 / ATA5577 configuration-register decode) — ✅ shipped v0.465.0 — decodes the **T5577 block-0 configuration register**, the 32-bit word that controls how an LF 125 kHz tag modulates/clocks its data. The T5577 is the ubiquitous rewritable LF blank used to clone EM4100 / HID Prox / Indala / AWID; decoding its config block surfaces the **modulation** (Direct / FSK1/2/1a/2a / ASK-Manchester / PSK1/2/3 / Biphase / Biphase-a), **data bit rate** (RF/8…RF/128), PSK clock, AOR, **max block**, password-enabled and sequence-terminator flags — the diagnostic complement to the project's T5577 clone/write tooling (`rfid_write` / `loader_t5577_multiwriter`, which set these configs but couldn't read them back). The bit layout, bit-rate table, and the modulation value→name table are taken from the **Proxmark3 reference** (`doc/T5577_Guide.md` + `client cmdlft55xx.c GetModulationStr`) and verified byte-for-byte against **two real config words**: `0x00148040` (EM4100 → RF/64, Manchester, 2 blocks) and `0x00107060` (HID Prox → RF/50, FSK2a, 3 blocks). (Verification caught a recalled error — PSK1 is modulation value 1, not 0x0A.) No confidently-wrong output: a modulation value outside the documented set is surfaced as a numeric code, and extended mode (master key 0x6/0x9, which reinterprets the reserved bits) is flagged rather than mis-decoded. Verified against both vectors + constructed PSK/flags / extended-mode / error cases; fuzz-clean (4.2M execs, 0 panics). Native — fixed bit-field extraction, stdlib only (`internal/t55xx`).
- `presco_encode` (Presco block generator) — ✅ shipped v0.620.0 — the inverse of `presco_decode` (v0.612), **completing the LF clone-generation set** (`em4100_encode`, `rfid_pacs_encode`, `ioprox_encode`, `jablotron_encode`, `viking_encode`, `noralsy_encode`): builds the 128-bit Presco LF block from a 32-bit full code — the 0x10D00000 preamble, two zero words, then the full code (site code = (full>>24)&0xFF, user code = full&0xFFFF). Presco carries no checksum, so the structural frame is the integrity gate. **No confidently-wrong output**: the layout is the same one `presco_decode` uses (from the Proxmark3 `cmdlfpresco.c` encoder/decoder, which are inverse), and this encoder **round-trips** with the decoder (decoding the emitted block reproduces the full code) and reproduces the reference vector (full code 0x07AABBCC → `10D00000000000000000000007AABBCC`). Generation only — transmits nothing and writes to no device, so it stays Low like the decoder. Verified by the vector match and a decode∘encode round-trip across several full codes; native — fixed-frame placement, stdlib only, no new go.mod dep (`internal/presco`). Registry 612→613.
- `presco_decode` (Presco 125 kHz LF access-control decode) — ✅ shipped v0.612.0 — decodes the **Presco** 128-bit LF data block, the credential format used by Presco readers (gate / garage / building access) — continuing the LF reader-cloning recon set alongside `ioprox_decode` / `jablotron_decode` / `viking_decode` / `noralsy_decode`. Recovers the **32-bit full code** and the Proxmark-style **site code** / **user code** derived from it. Input is the *decoded* 128-bit block — the bytes a Proxmark3 `lf presco demod` emits, MSB-first; the on-air ASK demodulation is the reader's job and out of scope, so the decode is deterministic. Layout: 32-bit 0x10D00000 preamble, two zero words, then the 32-bit full code (bits 96-127); site = (full>>24)&0xFF, user = full&0xFFFF. **No confidently-wrong output**: the layout is taken from the **Proxmark3** client (`cmdlfpresco.c`) — both its encoder (`getPrescoBits`) and decoder (`detectPresco`), which are inverse and so internally cross-check. Presco is a **single-reference** format here (the Flipper Zero mainline firmware does not implement it) and carries **no checksum**, so integrity rests on the 96-bit structural gate (preamble 0x10D00000 + two zero words, a ~1-in-2^96 marker) — a block that fails the gate is **rejected** rather than mis-decoded; the 32-bit full code is the unambiguous primary value (literally the final word after the gate), with site/user surfaced as the Proxmark derivation. Verified against three vectors constructed from the (confirmed) encode layout and independently checked (full 0x07AABBCC → site 7 / user 48076; plus 0x00000001 and 0x12345678), and bad-preamble / non-zero-filler structural rejections; fuzz-clean (~2.9M execs, 0 panics); race-clean. Native — a structural gate + a 32-bit field read, stdlib only, no new go.mod dep (`internal/presco`). Registry 605→606.
- `noralsy_encode` (Noralsy block generator) — ✅ shipped v0.619.0 — the inverse of `noralsy_decode` (v0.611): builds the 96-bit Noralsy LF block from a printed card number and year, completing the LF clone-generation set (`em4100_encode`, `rfid_pacs_encode`, `ioprox_encode`, `jablotron_encode`, `viking_encode`). BCD-encodes the card into the 28-bit packed field (placed non-contiguously per the layout), BCD-encodes the year, writes the 0xBB0214FF preamble, and computes the two 4-bit nibble checksums (chk1 over bits 32-71, chk2 over bits 0-75, the latter including chk1). **No confidently-wrong output**: the layout, the non-contiguous packing, the year field and the two checksums are the same Proxmark3-/Flipper-cross-verified ones `noralsy_decode` uses, and the encoder **round-trips** with it (decoding the emitted block reproduces the card number + year with both checksums valid) and reproduces the reference vector (card 1234567 / year 2021 → `BB0214FF1232104567370000`) — the strongest verification, no external vector needed. A card beyond 7 BCD digits, or a year outside the round-trippable 1961-2060 window (the decoder's >60 century-heuristic boundary), is rejected rather than emitting a block that re-decodes to a different year. Generation only — transmits nothing and writes to no device, so it stays Low like the decoder. Verified by the vector match and a decode∘encode round-trip across several card/year pairs incl. the 1999/2060 boundaries; native — BCD encode + non-contiguous placement + two nibble checksums, stdlib only, no new go.mod dep (`internal/noralsy`). Registry 611→612.
- `noralsy_decode` (Noralsy 125 kHz LF access-control decode) — ✅ shipped v0.611.0 — decodes the **Noralsy** 96-bit LF data block, the credential format used by Noralsy readers/fobs (common in **French / wider-EU** residential access control and intercom systems) — continuing the LF reader-cloning recon set alongside `ioprox_decode` / `jablotron_decode` / `viking_decode`, complementing the HID/Indala/AWID PACS coverage. Recovers the **card ID** and **manufacture year** and validates the two nibble **checksums**. Input is the *decoded* 96-bit block — the bytes a Proxmark3 `lf noralsy demod` / Flipper Zero LF read emits, MSB-first; the on-air ASK demodulation is the reader's job and out of scope, so the decode is deterministic. Layout: 32-bit 0xBB0214FF preamble, a 28-bit card ID packed across bits 32-43/56-63/64-71, an 8-bit BCD year (bits 44-51), and two 4-bit nibble-XOR checksums (chk1 over bits 32-71, chk2 over bits 0-75). **No confidently-wrong output**: the bit layout, the (non-contiguous) card-ID assembly masks, the year field and the two checksums are taken from — and **agree byte-for-byte between two independent reference implementations**, the **Proxmark3** client (`cmdlfnoralsy.c` `noralsy_chksum` + the cardid/year assembly) and the **Flipper Zero firmware** (`protocol_noralsy.c`); the 32-bit 0xBB0214FF preamble is a hard structural gate (a ~1-in-4-billion marker) — a block without it is **rejected** rather than mis-decoded; the two checksums are surfaced as `chk1_valid`/`chk2_valid` (a frame whose checksums fail is reported, never asserted as a real credential). The references **disagree only on card-ID presentation** (Proxmark BCD-decimal vs Flipper hex), so **both** are surfaced (`card_id_bcd` + `card_id_hex`, with a `card_id_is_bcd` flag) rather than asserting one; and the 19xx/20xx century split is a documented heuristic, noted as such. Verified against three vectors constructed from the spec and independently checked (card 1234567 / year 2021; a year-1999 case; and a non-BCD card flagging `card_id_is_bcd:false`), a checksum-mismatch case and a bad-preamble structural rejection; fuzz-clean (~3.3M execs, 0 panics); race-clean. Native — fixed bit extraction + two nibble checksums, stdlib only, no new go.mod dep (`internal/noralsy`). Registry 604→605.
- `viking_encode` (Viking block generator) — ✅ shipped v0.618.0 — the inverse of `viking_decode` (v0.610): builds the 64-bit Viking ('Viking Acs') LF block from a 32-bit card ID, extending the LF clone-generation set (`em4100_encode`, `rfid_pacs_encode`, `ioprox_encode`, `jablotron_encode`). Builds the 24-bit 0xF20000 preamble + the 32-bit card ID + the 8-bit checksum set so the XOR of all eight bytes equals 0xA8. **No confidently-wrong output**: the layout + checksum are the same Proxmark3-/Flipper-cross-verified ones `viking_decode` uses, and the encoder **round-trips** with it (decoding the emitted block reproduces the card ID) and reproduces the reference vector (card 0x0001A337 → `F200000001A337CF`) — the strongest verification, no external vector needed. Generation only — transmits nothing and writes to no device, so it stays Low like the decoder. Verified by the vector match and a decode∘encode round-trip across several card IDs; native — fixed bit placement + an XOR checksum, stdlib only, no new go.mod dep (`internal/viking`). Registry 610→611.
- `viking_decode` (Viking 125 kHz LF access-control decode) — ✅ shipped v0.610.0 — decodes the **Viking** ('Viking Acs') 64-bit LF data block, continuing the LF reader-cloning recon set alongside `ioprox_decode` / `jablotron_decode`, complementing `em4100_decode` / `fdxb_decode` and the HID/Indala/AWID PACS coverage. Recovers the **32-bit card ID** and validates the **8-bit checksum**. Input is the *decoded* 64-bit block — the bytes a Proxmark3 `lf viking demod` / Flipper Zero LF read emits, MSB-first; the on-air ASK demodulation is the reader's job and out of scope, so the decode is deterministic. Layout: 24-bit 0xF20000 preamble, 32-bit card ID (bits 24-55), 8-bit checksum (bits 56-63) defined so the XOR of all eight bytes equals 0xA8. **No confidently-wrong output**: the bit layout and checksum are taken from — and **agree byte-for-byte between two independent reference implementations**, the **Proxmark3** client (`cmdlfviking.c` `getVikingBits` / `isValidVikingChecksum`) and the **Flipper Zero firmware** (`protocol_viking.c`); the 0xF20000 preamble is a hard structural gate — a block without it is **rejected** rather than mis-decoded; the checksum is surfaced as `crc_valid` (a structurally-valid block that fails it is reported, never asserted as a real credential). Native — fixed bit extraction + an XOR checksum, stdlib only, no new go.mod dep (`internal/viking`). Registry 603→604.
- `jablotron_encode` (Jablotron block generator) — ✅ shipped v0.617.0 — the inverse of `jablotron_decode` (v0.609): builds the 64-bit Jablotron LF block from a printed card number, extending the LF clone-generation set (`em4100_encode`, `rfid_pacs_encode`, `ioprox_encode`). BCD-encodes the card number into the five card-data bytes (the inverse of the decoder's render), prepends the 0xFFFF preamble and appends the 8-bit checksum (sum of the card bytes XOR 0x3A). **No confidently-wrong output**: the layout + checksum are the same Proxmark3-/Flipper-cross-verified ones `jablotron_decode` uses, and the encoder **round-trips** with it (decoding the emitted block reproduces the card number) and reproduces the reference vector (card 1234567890 → `FFFF12345678909E`) — the strongest verification, no external vector needed. A card number beyond ten BCD digits is rejected. Generation only — transmits nothing and writes to no device, so it stays Low like the decoder. Verified by the vector match and a decode∘encode round-trip across several card numbers; native — BCD encode + a checksum, stdlib only, no new go.mod dep (`internal/jablotron`). Registry 609→610.
- `jablotron_decode` (Jablotron 125 kHz LF access-control decode) — ✅ shipped v0.609.0 — decodes the **Jablotron** 64-bit LF data block, the credential format used by Jablotron readers/fobs (widely deployed across **Czech / Slovak / wider-EU** access control and intercom systems) — continuing the LF reader-cloning recon set alongside `ioprox_decode`, complementing `em4100_decode` / `fdxb_decode` and the HID/Indala/AWID PACS coverage. Recovers the **40-bit card data**, renders the **printed card number** (BCD), and validates the **8-bit checksum** (`(sum of the five card bytes mod 256) XOR 0x3A`). Input is the *decoded* 64-bit block — the bytes a Proxmark3 `lf jablotron demod` / Flipper Zero LF read emits, MSB-first; the on-air ASK demodulation is the reader's job and out of scope, so the decode is deterministic. Layout: 16-bit 0xFFFF preamble, 40-bit card data (bits 16-55), 8-bit checksum (bits 56-63); the printed number reads each card byte as two BCD digits, the five concatenated base-100. **No confidently-wrong output**: the bit layout, the checksum and the BCD render are taken from — and **agree byte-for-byte between two independent reference implementations**, the **Proxmark3** client (`cmdlfjablotron.c` `jablontron_chksum` / `getJablontronCardId`) and the **Flipper Zero firmware** (`protocol_jablotron.c`); the 0xFFFF preamble is a hard structural gate — a block without it is **rejected** rather than mis-decoded; the checksum is surfaced as `crc_valid` (a structurally-valid block that fails it is reported, never asserted as a real credential); and when the card data is not valid BCD the printed-number field is flagged (`card_id_is_bcd:false`) and the raw 40-bit value is relied upon. Verified against two vectors constructed from the spec and independently checked (card bytes `12 34 56 78 90` → printed 1234567890, checksum 0x9E; plus a non-BCD case `00 01 B6 69 01`), a checksum-mismatch case and a missing-preamble structural rejection; fuzz-clean (~3.5M execs, 0 panics); race-clean. Native — fixed bit extraction + checksum + BCD render, stdlib only, no new go.mod dep (`internal/jablotron`). Registry 602→603.
- `ansible_vault_decode` (Ansible Vault crack-triage) — ✅ shipped v0.681.0 — a crack-triage sibling of `zip_crack_triage` / `kdbx_decode` for the **DevOps / infrastructure-as-code** domain. Ansible Vault (`$ANSIBLE_VAULT;…`) is the standard at-rest encryption for secrets in Ansible playbooks/roles, so a vault file is **common loot** on a CI/ops host or in a pulled repo — and its password protects **every** value inside. The new **`internal/ansiblevault`** package parses the header offline and reports the **version** (1.1 / 1.2), the **cipher** (AES256), the optional **vault-id** (the key label, useful for targeting), the envelope size, and the matching **hashcat mode (16900)**. **No confidently-wrong output**: the file is recognised only by its `$ANSIBLE_VAULT;` magic header; it reports the **envelope parameters only**; and a non-vault input is rejected. The KDF is **PBKDF2-HMAC-SHA256 (10000 iterations)**, a slow per-guess target. **Wrap-vs-native: native** — a header parse over the documented format (ansible `lib/ansible/parsing/vault/__init__.py`), **no new go.mod dep**. Verified against **real ansible-core VaultLib output** — a 1.1 header and a 1.2 header with a vault-id — plus malformed-header / non-vault rejections; fuzz-clean; lint/vet/full-suite green. `internal/ansiblevault`. Registry 651→652.
  - **Follow-up v0.682.0** — now also rebuilds the **ready-to-crack `ansible2john` hash** (`$ansible$0*0*salt*hmac*ciphertext`, the **`john_hash`** field) **offline**, completing the stated purpose ("feeds straight into the hashcat tooling" — previously only the *mode* was emitted, with nothing to feed it). The body is `hexlify(hexlify(salt) + "\n" + hmac + "\n" + hexlify(ciphertext))` (VaultAES256.encrypt), so a single hex-decode yields the three fields verbatim. The rebuild is **structurally guarded** (32-byte salt + 32-byte HMAC-SHA256 → 64 hex chars each, valid-hex ciphertext, AES256-only) and emits **no** hash on any deviation rather than a wrong one. Ground-truth tested: the exact `ansible2john` string for a real `ansible-vault encrypt` file, plus malformed-body → empty-hash cases; fuzz-clean (3.8M execs). This is the **one** crack-triage tool where emitting the actual hash is feasible offline — `zip_crack_triage` / `pdf_crack_triage` / `kdbx_decode` need binary fields not present in their inputs, so they correctly remain mode-only.
- `pkcs12_decode` (PKCS#12 / PFX forensic triage) — ✅ shipped v0.685.0 — triages a **PKCS#12 / PFX** keystore (`.p12` / `.pfx`), the **modern sibling of `jks_decode`**: keytool's **default since Java 9**, the format **Windows** exports TLS identities into, and the container for **mobile provisioning**. The new **`internal/pkcs12`** package does a **bounded `encoding/asn1` walk** of the documented PKCS#12 structure (**RFC 7292**) and reports the **version**, the **MAC parameters** (algorithm / salt / iterations — the integrity digest is the **`pfx2john` crack target**), each top-level **safe** classified **plaintext vs password-encrypted** (with the PBE algorithm named), the **certificate identities** found in **plaintext** bags via stdlib **`crypto/x509`** (subject / issuer / expiry / self-signed), and whether any private key is stored **unshrouded** (no per-key encryption — recoverable from the container **without** the password, surfaced as a WARNING). **No confidently-wrong output**: recognised only as a well-formed PFX (version + `pkcs7-data` authSafe); ASN.1 parsed with stdlib `encoding/asn1` (**errors, not panics**, on malformed input); a certificate that fails to parse is recorded with its error, never asserted valid; password-encrypted safes are reported **encrypted**, never guessed; it does **not** crack/decrypt/recover any key or password. **Wrap-vs-native: native** — a bounded `encoding/asn1` walk of RFC 7292 + stdlib `crypto/x509`, **no new go.mod dep**. **Anchored to real `openssl`-generated `.p12` files** — a plaintext-certBag store, a default (encrypted-certBag, **PBES2**) store, and a fully-unshrouded store — with MAC params / cert subjects / safe classification / the unshrouded-key finding all cross-checked against `openssl pkcs12 -info`; fuzz-clean (~0.13M execs, 0 panics); lint/vet/full-suite green. Pairs with `jks_decode` / `x509_certificate_decode` / `pem_privkey_decode` (the key-forensics set). `internal/pkcs12`. Registry 654→655.
- `jks_decode` (Java KeyStore forensic triage) — ✅ shipped v0.684.0 — triages a **Java KeyStore** (`.jks` / `.keystore`), classic **enterprise loot**: Java app servers (Tomcat, JBoss, custom services) keep their **TLS server keys** and **code-signing keys** in one, protected by a store password. The new **`internal/jks`** package does a **bounds-checked** parse of the documented Sun JKS binary container (`sun.security.provider.JavaKeyStore`) and reports the **version** (1 / 2), every **entry** (alias, type — private-key vs trusted-cert — and creation time), the **certificate identities** parsed with stdlib `crypto/x509` (subject / issuer / expiry / self-signed — **whose** TLS or signing identity each key is), and the matching **crack mode (hashcat -m 15500, `keystore2john`)**. The store password is a slow SHA-1-keyed digest; recovering it then decrypts the private keys, granting the server's own identity. **No confidently-wrong output**: recognised only by its **`0xFEEDFEED` magic** and a known version; **every field read is bounds-checked** and a structural deviation (truncation, an over-long length, a missing 20-byte trailer) is reported as an error, never guessed (the OOM-safe reader pattern from the rpc-framing fix); a certificate that fails to parse is recorded with its error, never asserted valid; it does **not** crack/decrypt/recover any key or password; a trusted-cert-only store is noted as having **no** `-m 15500` target. **Wrap-vs-native: native** — a bounds-checked reader over the documented format + stdlib `crypto/x509`, **no new go.mod dep**. **Anchored to a real `keytool`-generated keystore** (a two-private-key store — `CN=app.example.com` + `CN=Code Signing` — and a trusted-cert store, with aliases/types/subjects cross-checked against `keytool -list`), plus a hand-built **version-1** vector (the no-per-cert-type layout), graceful cert-parse-failure, and magic / version / truncation / missing-trailer rejections; fuzz-clean (~0.45M execs, 0 panics); lint/vet/full-suite green. Pairs with `x509_certificate_decode` / `ssh_privkey_decode` / `pem_privkey_decode` (the key-forensics set). `internal/jks`. Registry 653→654.
- `shadow_decode` (/etc/shadow credential triage) — ✅ shipped v0.686.0 — parses a looted Linux **`/etc/shadow`**, the **single highest-value Linux post-exploitation artifact** (every local account's password hash). The new **`internal/shadow`** package classifies, **per user**, the password field: the **crypt scheme** (sha512crypt `$6$` / sha256crypt `$5$` / md5crypt `$1$` / bcrypt `$2*$` / yescrypt `$y$` / gost-yescrypt / sha1crypt / SunMD5 / apr1 / **descrypt** 13-char DES), the matching **crack mode** (hashcat **`-m`** + John format) to feed the cracker, the account **status** (active / locked / no-password / disabled / shadowed), and the password-aging fields — plus per-file counts of **crackable**, **locked**, and **no-password** accounts. **No confidently-wrong output**: classified only by the documented crypt id or shape; an unrecognised field is **unknown** with no crack mode, never guessed; a hashcat mode is emitted only where hashcat has a **native** mode (yescrypt / gost-yescrypt reported **John-only**); a **locked** account whose hash is still present (`!$6$…`) is flagged **locked _and_ crackable** (the lock only disables login); a passwd-style `x` is reported **shadowed**, not a hash; input with no shadow-shaped line is rejected. **Wrap-vs-native: native** — a field split over shadow(5) / crypt(5), **no new go.mod dep**; crack modes per the hashcat / John format tables. **Anchored to real `openssl`-generated crypt hashes** (`$6$` / `$5$` / `$1$`) plus published bcrypt / yescrypt / descrypt vectors, covering every scheme + locked-but-hashed + no-password + disabled + passwd-redirect + rejection paths; fuzz-clean; lint/vet/full-suite green. Pairs with `hash_identify` / `sha512crypt` / `bcrypt` (the per-hash crackers). `internal/shadow`. Registry 655→656.
- `rclone_config_decode` (rclone-config credential extractor) — ✅ shipped v0.683.0 — extracts **cloud-remote credentials** from an **rclone config** (`rclone.conf`), common loot on a **backup / sync / CI host** where each `[remote]` holds the credentials for an S3 bucket, a Google Drive, an SFTP/WebDAV server, and so on. The uniquely recoverable part is rclone's **obscured passwords**: rclone does **not** store them in plaintext, but it does **not** encrypt them with a user secret either — it "obscures" them with **AES-256-CTR under a single hardcoded key** (explicitly **not** a security measure, per rclone's own docs), so they are **fully reversible offline**. The new **`internal/rcloneconfig`** package reveals the `pass` / `password` / `password2` fields and surfaces the **plaintext secrets** (S3 `access_key_id` / `secret_access_key`, OAuth `token`, `client_secret`, …) verbatim, per remote with its `type`. **No confidently-wrong output**: a password is revealed only when its value is a valid obscure blob that decodes to **printable** text; a value that does not decode, or decodes to non-printable bytes, is reported **plaintext** / **unrevealable** with the raw surfaced — never a garbage "password"; non-rclone input is rejected. **Wrap-vs-native: native** — an INI scanner + rclone's exact reveal transform (`crypto/aes` + `crypto/cipher`, **no new go.mod dep**); the key and algorithm are taken verbatim from rclone `fs/config/obscure/obscure.go` and **anchored to rclone's own three published reveal vectors** (`YWFh…XMaGgIlEQ`→`potato`, etc.), plus round-trip / plaintext-downgrade / non-printable-not-claimed / rejection tests; fuzz-clean (~2.4M execs, 0 panics); lint/vet/full-suite green. Pairs with `vpn_config_decode` / `wifi_config_decode` / `bluez_pairing_decode` (the host-loot extraction set). `internal/rcloneconfig`. Registry 652→653.
- `vpn_config_decode` (VPN-config credential extractor) — ✅ shipped v0.680.0 — extracts **VPN credentials** from a host config, among the **highest-value loot artifacts**: the WireGuard interface **PrivateKey**, or an OpenVPN client **key / inline username+password**, grants the operator the host's own VPN access — a **direct pivot onto the internal network** it tunnels to, no cracking required. Recognises **WireGuard `.conf`** (wg-quick) and **OpenVPN `.ovpn`**. For WireGuard it reports the interface PrivateKey, address, DNS, and each **[Peer]** (public key, preshared key, endpoint, allowed IPs); for OpenVPN the **remote** server(s), protocol, the **auth method** (certificate / user-pass / both), whether a client **private key is embedded** (`<key>` PEM block), and any **inline username+password**. The credential is the **explicit extraction goal**, so the key / password **is** surfaced verbatim (a `has_credential` flag summarises). The new **`internal/vpnconfig`** package uses a minimal INI scanner for WireGuard and a directive/inline-block scanner for OpenVPN — **no new go.mod dep**. **No confidently-wrong output**: the format is detected by its unambiguous syntax; a credential is surfaced when present and reported absent otherwise; and input matching neither format is rejected. (Distinct from `wireguard_packet_decode`, which decodes the WireGuard wire protocol — this parses the *config file*.) Verified against a WireGuard config (interface key + a peer with endpoint/preshared-key), an OpenVPN certificate config (two remotes + embedded `<ca>`/`<cert>`/`<key>`), and an OpenVPN inline user-pass config; fuzz-clean (~4.9M execs, 0 panics); lint/vet/full-suite green. `internal/vpnconfig`. Registry 650→651.
- `bluez_pairing_decode` (Bluetooth pairing-key extractor) — ✅ shipped v0.679.0 — the **BLE/Bluetooth analogue of `wifi_config_decode`**: a foothold on a Linux host yields, for every device it has bonded with, the long-term cryptographic material from the **BlueZ device `info` file** (`/var/lib/bluetooth/<adapter>/<device>/info`) — the **BR/EDR LinkKey**, and for LE the **LongTermKey (LTK)**, **IdentityResolvingKey (IRK)**, and signing keys (CSRK). With these an operator can **decrypt sniffed traffic** for the bonded link, **resolve a device's resolvable-private address** (IRK), or **impersonate** the bonded device — directly useful alongside a BLE sniffer or the Flipper's BLE radio. The new **`internal/bluezkeys`** package reports the device name, transport (BR/EDR / LE / dual), address type, and each recovered key with its parameters (LTK EncSize/EDiv/Rand, LinkKey type), handling both the modern `[PeripheralLongTermKey]` and the pre-5.x `[SlaveLongTermKey]` group names. The keys are the **explicit extraction goal**, so they are surfaced **verbatim** (the device MAC is the info file's directory name — supplied by path, not in the file). **No confidently-wrong output**: the file is recognised only by a BlueZ-specific key section; an unpaired / cleared file (no key sections) is **rejected**, not guessed; non-BlueZ input is rejected. **Wrap-vs-native: native** — a minimal GKeyFile (INI) scanner over the documented BlueZ storage format (`doc/settings-storage.txt`; `src/device.c`), **no new go.mod dep**. Verified against a dual-mode bond (LinkKey + LTK + IRK), a pure-LE bond using the legacy `[SlaveLongTermKey]` name, a BR/EDR-only bond, and no-key / non-BlueZ rejections; fuzz-clean (~4.7M execs, 0 panics); lint/vet/full-suite green. `internal/bluezkeys`. Registry 649→650.
- `wifi_config_decode` (stored-WiFi-credential extractor) — ✅ shipped v0.677.0 — the **host-side complement to the project's RF-side WiFi tooling**: once an operator has a foothold on a host, its saved WiFi configs hand over the **pre-shared keys directly** — no handshake capture or cracking required. Recognises the three standard formats: **wpa_supplicant.conf** (Linux / embedded / routers), **NetworkManager `.nmconnection`** keyfiles (Linux desktop), and the **Windows `netsh wlan export profile` XML**. For each network it reports the **SSID**, the security type (WPA-PSK / WPA-EAP / WEP / OPEN), the recovered **PSK** (passphrase or 64-hex PMK), and for enterprise networks the EAP method + **identity** + password. Because the recovered key is the **explicit extraction goal**, the passphrase **is** surfaced verbatim (unlike the credential-*container* decoders that only flag a secret's presence — here the key is the loot). The new **`internal/wificonfig`** package uses hand scanners for the wpa_supplicant block syntax and the NetworkManager INI plus stdlib `encoding/xml` for the Windows profile — **no new go.mod dep**. **No confidently-wrong output**: the format is detected by its unambiguous syntax; a Windows key stored **DPAPI-`protected`** is reported as **encrypted** (the plaintext is *not* invented, and it does not count toward the credential tally); an open network is reported key-less; and input matching none of the three formats is rejected. Verified against a multi-network wpa_supplicant.conf (WPA-PSK passphrase, open, hidden WPA-EAP/PEAP with identity+password, 64-hex PMK), a NetworkManager PSK keyfile + an enterprise `[802-1x]` variant, and Windows XML in both plaintext and DPAPI-protected forms; fuzz-clean (~3.5M execs, 0 panics); lint/vet/full-suite green. `internal/wificonfig`. Registry 648→649. **Extended v0.678.0** with the **Android `WifiConfigStore.xml`** (`/data/misc/wifi/`, the per-`<Network>` `<string name="SSID">`/`<string name="PreSharedKey">` schema) and the **OpenWrt `/etc/config/wireless`** UCI file (`config wifi-iface` blocks → `option ssid`/`option key`/`option encryption`; `config wifi-device` radio blocks skipped) — the two highest-frequency remaining loot sources (rooted phones, router pivots), bringing it to **five formats**. Verified against an Android store with a PSK + an open network and an OpenWrt config with a psk2 AP + an open guest SSID; fuzz-clean. Registry unchanged (an enhancement, not a new tool).
- `crack_triage` (unified crack-triage front-end) — ✅ shipped v0.676.0 — the **single entry point over the crack-triage suite** (`kdbx_decode` + `zip_crack_triage` + `pdf_crack_triage`), the crack-triage analogue of the project's `secret_identify` / `hash_identify` / `email_auth_decode` routers. Hand it **any encrypted loot file** of unknown type and it **detects the artifact by its magic bytes** — KeePass `.kdbx` (`0x9AA2D903 0xB54BFB67`), ZIP (`PK\x03\x04`/`PK\x05\x06`/`PK\x07\x08`), or PDF (`%PDF-`) — and **routes to the matching in-tree decoder**, returning the artifact type, the **hashcat mode** that cracks it, and the full per-format detail (cipher, KDF / key-derivation cost, AES strength, …). So an operator (or the agent) with a mixed loot dump gets one answer to *"what is this, and how do I crack it?"* without pre-classifying the file. The new **`internal/cracktriage`** package is **native orchestration** over `internal/kdbx` + `internal/ziptriage` + `internal/pdftriage` — **no new go.mod dep**. **No confidently-wrong output**: detection is by unambiguous file-format magic, so a file is **never mis-routed**; an unrecognised type is **rejected**; each routed decoder keeps its own *parameters-only* guarantee (nothing cracked, decrypted, or emitted as a john/hashcat hash); a `hashcat_mode` of `0` means the file is not password-protected. Input is the file base64-encoded (or hex). Verified by magic-byte detection for all three types + an unrecognised-input rejection, and end-to-end routing of a real KDBX4 header (→ 13400), a real ZipCrypto archive (→ 17210), and an unencrypted PDF (→ mode 0); fuzz-clean; lint/vet/full-suite green. `internal/cracktriage`. Registry 647→648.
- `pdf_crack_triage` (encrypted-PDF crack triage) — ✅ shipped v0.675.0 — completes the **crack-triage suite** alongside `kdbx_decode` and `zip_crack_triage`: triages a password-protected PDF for cracking. Encrypted PDFs (financial, legal, scanned documents) are among the most common high-value loot artifacts, and the answer to *"can I crack this, and with which hashcat mode?"* is fully determined by the PDF **Standard security handler**'s algorithm (`/V`), revision (`/R`), key length, and — for `/V 4` — the crypt-filter method (`/CFM`). The new **`internal/pdftriage`** package reads the Standard security dictionary and maps it to the cipher + **hashcat mode**: RC4-40 (R2) → `10400`, RC4-128 (R3, or R4 with `/V2`) → `10500`, AES-128 (R4 with `/AESV2`) → `10600`, AES-256 (R5/R6, `/AESV3`) → `10700`; it also surfaces the PDF version and the `/P` permission flags. The PDF spec (ISO 32000-1 §7.6.1) requires the `/Encrypt` dictionary to be a **direct, uncompressed object**, so reading it from the raw bytes is reliable (not a heuristic). **No confidently-wrong output**: it reports the encryption **parameters only** — it does **not** crack, decrypt, or emit the `pdf2john` hash; a PDF with **no `/Encrypt`** is reported as not password-protected (nothing to crack); a non-Standard (public-key, e.g. `Adobe.PubSec`) handler is **named but not given a password hashcat mode**; and non-PDF input is rejected. The nested `/CF /StdCF /Length` (key **bytes**) is correctly distinguished from the dictionary `/Length` (key **bits**) by taking the larger. **Wrap-vs-native: native** — a byte scan over the documented PDF encryption dictionary, **no new go.mod dep**. Input is the `.pdf` file base64-encoded (or hex). Verified against **real pikepdf/qpdf-generated encrypted PDFs** — V4/R4 RC4-128 → 10500, V4/R4 AES-128 (`/AESV2`) → 10600, V5/R6 AES-256 (`/AESV3`) → 10700 — plus a hand-built unencrypted control and non-PDF rejection; fuzz-clean (~6M execs, 0 panics); lint/vet/full-suite green. `internal/pdftriage`. Registry 646→647.
- `zip_crack_triage` (encrypted-ZIP crack triage) — ✅ shipped v0.673.0 — the **crack-triage sibling of `kdbx_decode`**: triages a password-protected ZIP for cracking. Encrypted archives are among the most common high-value loot artifacts, and the operator's first question is *"can I crack this, and with which hashcat mode?"* — which hinges entirely on **how** the ZIP is encrypted: legacy **ZipCrypto** (PKWARE traditional) is a weak, fast target (with a known-plaintext break), whereas **WinZip AES** (128/192/256-bit) is a slow PBKDF2-HMAC-SHA1 target. The new **`internal/ziptriage`** package walks the ZIP **central directory** (located via the End-Of-Central-Directory record) and reports the encryption **scheme**, the **AES strength + AE version** when applicable, the **encrypted-entry count**, the first encrypted filename, and the matching **hashcat mode** — `13600` for WinZip AES; the `17200`/`17210`/`17220`/`17225` family for ZipCrypto, selected by compression + file count. **No confidently-wrong output**: it reports the archive's **encryption structure only** — it does **not** crack, decrypt, or emit the `zip2john` hash (that needs the encrypted record bytes and is out of scope); an archive with **no encrypted entries** is reported as not password-protected (nothing to crack), and non-ZIP input is rejected. **Wrap-vs-native: native** — `encoding/binary` over the documented ZIP APPNOTE central directory + the WinZip AES `0x9901` extra field, **no new go.mod dep**. Input is the `.zip` file base64-encoded (or hex). Verified against **real archives** — `zip -P` (ZipCrypto, stored → 17210) and `7z -mem=AES256` (WinZip AE-2, 256-bit → 13600) — plus a stdlib-built unencrypted archive (no-password path) and non-ZIP/short rejections; fuzz-clean (~7M execs, 0 panics); lint/vet/full-suite green. `internal/ziptriage`. Registry 645→646.
- `kdbx_decode` (KeePass .kdbx header → crack triage) — ✅ shipped v0.672.0 — decodes the outer header of a **KeePass database** (`.kdbx`) into the facts that decide whether it is worth attacking, extending the credential-forensics set toward **crack triage** (pairs with `hash_identify` and the hashcat tooling). A `.kdbx` is one of the highest-value loot artifacts, and its crackability hinges almost entirely on the **key-derivation function**: a legacy **AES-KDF** database with a low transform-round count is tractable, whereas a modern **KDBX4** file using memory-hard **Argon2** (e.g. 64 MiB × 14 iterations) throttles GPU cracking by orders of magnitude. The new **`internal/kdbx`** package parses the outer header (validating the `0x9AA2D903 0xB54BFB67` magic, handling both the KDBX3 uint16-size and KDBX4 uint32-size TLV layouts, and the KDBX4 **VariantDictionary** KdfParameters) and reports the **version**, the **cipher** (AES-256 / ChaCha20 / Twofish by UUID), the **KDF** (AES-KDF / Argon2d / Argon2id by UUID) with its **cost** (AES-KDF transform rounds, or Argon2 iterations / memory / parallelism), a qualitative crack-difficulty note, and the **hashcat mode (13400)**. **No confidently-wrong output**: an unknown cipher/KDF UUID is surfaced as **raw hex** rather than guessed; it parses the **outer header only** — it does **not** derive the master key, decrypt the database, or emit the `keepass2john` `$keepass$` hash (that needs the encrypted payload and is out of scope), so it surfaces crack *cost*, never a claim the database is crackable; malformed input is rejected. **Wrap-vs-native: native** — `encoding/binary` over the documented KDBX header + KDBX4 VariantDictionary, **no new go.mod dep**. Input is the `.kdbx` file base64-encoded (or hex). Verified against a **real pykeepass-generated KDBX4 file** (AES-256 / gzip / Argon2d I=14, M=64 MiB, P=2, cross-checked by an independent Python VariantDictionary decode), a spec-faithful KDBX3 header (AES-KDF transform rounds), the unknown-cipher raw-hex path, and bad-magic / short / truncated rejections; fuzz-clean (~7.2M execs, 0 panics); lint/vet/full-suite green. `internal/kdbx`. Registry 644→645.
- `secret_scan` (bulk secret scanner over the decoder suite) — ✅ shipped v0.670.0 — the **capstone over the credential-decoder suite** (`secret_identify` + `aws_key_decode` / `github_token_decode` / `gcp_service_account_decode` / `pypi_token_decode` / `jwt_decode` / …): where `secret_identify` classifies a single captured string, `secret_scan` is the real bulk loot-triage workflow — hand it a config, an `.env`, a log, an `env` dump, or a source blob and it **extracts every candidate credential and routes each through `secret_identify`**, reporting the type, the **line** it sits on, and (where the format carries a checksum/structure) whether it **validates**. The in-tree analogue of trufflehog / gitleaks, built on the project's own decoders. Extracts only **high-signal structural patterns** — PEM / OpenSSH / PGP key blocks, JWTs, AWS access key IDs, GitHub tokens, Google API keys / OAuth tokens, and the documented vendor-token prefixes (built from the same `vendorPrefixes` table `secret_identify` uses, so the two stay in sync) — then keeps a finding **only when `secret_identify` recognises the candidate**. **No confidently-wrong output**: a finding is always a **format match**, never an entropy guess; the **secret value is redacted** (prefix + length, never the full secret); findings carry their `validated` status; output is bounded with explicit `truncated` signalling; and the result notes that **absence of a finding is not proof a blob is clean** (only the high-signal patterns are extracted). **Wrap-vs-native: native** — stdlib `regexp` extraction + the in-tree `secretid.Identify`, **no new go.mod dep**. Verified against a multi-type blob (AWS key on a known line, valid-checksum GitHub token, JWT, Slack token, OpenSSH key block — each found and typed; clean text yields nothing), redaction (the full secret never appears in output), line-number accuracy, and empty input; fuzz-clean. `internal/secretid`. Registry 643→644.
- `dockercfg_decode` (Docker registry config → registries + usernames) — ✅ shipped v0.669.0 — decodes a **Docker registry credential config** into the registries it authenticates to and the username each entry carries, a natural pairing with `kubeconfig_decode` (the Kubernetes `kubernetes.io/dockerconfigjson` **image-pull secret** is exactly this format). Registry creds turn up constantly in loot — `~/.docker/config.json`, the legacy `.dockercfg`, and the k8s pull secret — and a credential with **push** access is a **supply-chain primitive** (publish a malicious image tag). The new **`internal/dockercfg`** package handles the modern `config.json` and the legacy `.dockercfg`; for each registry it reports the host, the **username** (from the base64 `auth` field), whether a **password** or an **identity token** is present, and any per-registry **credential helper**, and summarises whether the config carries embedded credentials vs deferring to an external `credHelpers` / `credsStore`. **No confidently-wrong output**: it reports the registry, username, and credential **shape** only — it **does not emit the decoded password** (presence is flagged, the secret is not echoed), never contacts a registry, and never asserts the credential is live; a malformed `auth` field is flagged rather than treated as a credential, and input that is not a recognisable Docker config is **rejected**. **Wrap-vs-native: native** — `encoding/json` + `encoding/base64` over the documented docker/cli config schema, **no new go.mod dep**. `secret_identify` routes a config carrying `auths`/`credsStore`/`credHelpers` to a full decode. Verified against a modern multi-registry config (base64 auth → username, an identity-token registry, a `credHelpers`-only registry, a global `credsStore`), a legacy `.dockercfg`, a creds-store-only no-embedded case, a malformed-auth flag, and unrelated-JSON rejection; fuzz-clean (~5.4M execs, 0 panics); lint/vet/full-suite green. `internal/dockercfg`. Registry 642→643.
- `kubeconfig_decode` (Kubernetes kubeconfig → attack surface) — ✅ shipped v0.668.0 — decodes a **Kubernetes kubeconfig** into the attack surface it exposes, extending the credential / cloud-forensics set (`gcp_service_account_decode` / `aws_key_decode` / `secret_identify`) into the project's **k8s / infra** domain. A kubeconfig is one of the highest-value artifacts in a cluster pentest or incident — it bundles the API endpoint(s) and frequently a **directly-usable credential** (an embedded client key, a bearer token, or a basic-auth password). The new **`internal/kubeconfig`** package reports each **cluster** (API server URL, TLS-verify posture, CA presence), each **user** reduced to its **credential kinds** (client-certificate / client-key / token / basic-auth / `exec:<plugin>` / `auth-provider:<name>`) with an **embedded-credential** flag — true for an embedded key/token/password (usable as-is), false for an `exec`/`auth-provider` that defers to the operator's own cloud login — and each **context** (cluster+user+namespace, current one marked); it summarises the **insecure clusters** (`insecure-skip-tls-verify`, a MITM foothold) and whether any embedded credential is present. **No confidently-wrong output**: it reports **structure and credential shape only** — never contacts an API server, never asserts a credential is live or its RBAC, and **does not emit the secret material** (presence is flagged, values are not); input that is not a `kind: Config` document is **rejected**, not guessed. **Wrap-vs-native: native** — `gopkg.in/yaml.v3` (already a **direct** dependency) over the documented kubeconfig (clientcmd `api.v1.Config`) schema, **no new go.mod dep**. Verified against a realistic multi-cluster config (embedded-key admin, token CI user, exec-plugin GKE user, an `insecure-skip-tls-verify` cluster, current-context flagging), an `auth-provider`-only no-embedded-credential case, and wrong-`kind` / non-YAML rejection; fuzz-clean (~0.6M execs, 0 panics); lint/vet/full-suite green. `internal/kubeconfig`. Registry 641→642.
- `macaroon_decode` (generic macaroon decoder) — ✅ shipped v0.667.0 — exposes the in-tree libmacaroons binary parser (`internal/macaroon`, shipped with `pypi_token_decode` in v0.665.0 and **gold-vector-verified** against the pymacaroons cross-implementation v1/v2 vectors) as a **generic tool for any macaroon**, not just PyPI tokens. Macaroons are Google-designed delegated-authorization credentials whose **caveats — the attenuations restricting the token — are readable without the issuing secret**; they back **Lightning L402 / LSAT** paid-API auth, PyPI tokens, Ubuntu SSO, and more. Decodes both the v1 packet and v2 binary serializations into location / identifier / caveats (each first- or third-party, ID as text-or-hex, vid hex) / signature, and accepts the **L402 header shape** (`L402 <macaroon>:<preimage>` / `LSAT …`), stripping the scheme and surfacing the preimage separately. **No confidently-wrong output**: asserts the macaroon's **structure only** — it does **not** verify the signature chain (needs the issuing root key, absent from a captured token) and never claims the token is valid/live; binary identifiers/caveats are shown as hex rather than mojibake. For a PyPI token (`pypi-…`) the dedicated `pypi_token_decode` additionally **interprets** the caveat JSON into a named scope. **Wrap-vs-native: native** — wraps `internal/macaroon` (stdlib only), **no new go.mod dep**. This fire also **closed the `internal/pypitoken` fuzz gap** (the one untested-parser hole of 290 decoder packages): added `FuzzDecode`, fuzz-clean (~3.7M execs, 0 panics). Verified by a handler-level test against the gold v2 vector, the L402 scheme/preimage split, and not-a-macaroon rejection; lint/vet/full-suite green. `internal/macaroon`. Registry 640→641.
- `gcp_service_account_decode` (GCP service-account JSON key → identity + blast radius) — ✅ shipped v0.666.0 — decodes a **Google Cloud service-account key** into whose identity it is and how much it can do, extending the credential-forensics set (`aws_key_decode` / `azure_sas_decode` / `pypi_token_decode` / `secret_identify`). A GCP SA key is the JSON file `gcloud iam service-accounts keys create` emits — among the **most damaging and most frequently-leaked cloud credentials** (a long-lived, often highly-privileged identity committed to repos, baked into CI, dropped in images). The new **`internal/gcpsakey`** package surfaces the identity fields (`project_id` / `client_email` / `client_id` / `private_key_id`); **classifies the account** by its email pattern — flagging the default **Compute Engine** (`…-compute@developer.gserviceaccount.com`) and **App Engine** (`…@appspot.gserviceaccount.com`) SAs, which carry the broad Editor role by default and so raise the impact; **parses the embedded PKCS#8 RSA private key** to confirm it is genuine + report its size and a SubjectPublicKeyInfo SHA-256 fingerprint; and runs the **ROCA (CVE-2017-15361)** weak-key test on the modulus (chaining `internal/roca`). **No confidently-wrong output**: it asserts the key's **structure and identity only** — never that the key is live or what IAM roles it holds (needs a GCP API call); a `private_key` that fails to parse is reported with `private_key_valid:false` (identity fields still surfaced), not asserted genuine; a JSON without the `service_account` shape is rejected. **Wrap-vs-native: native** — `encoding/json` + stdlib `crypto/x509` PKCS#8 parsing + `internal/roca`, **no new go.mod dep**; key-file schema is Google's documented format. Verification: the SPKI **fingerprint is anchored to an openssl-computed vector** (`openssl pkey -pubout -outform DER | openssl dgst -sha256`), plus user-managed / default-compute / default-appengine classification, the redacted-key graceful-downgrade path, and `service_account`-shape rejection; fuzz-clean (~2M execs, 0 panics); lint/vet/full-suite/govulncheck green. `secret_identify` routes a `service_account` JSON to a full decode + ROCA check. `internal/gcpsakey`. Registry 639→640.
- `pypi_token_decode` (PyPI API token → blast radius) — ✅ shipped v0.665.0 — decodes a **PyPI API token** (`pypi-…`) into the access it grants, extending the credential-forensics set (`secret_identify` / `github_token_decode` / `aws_key_decode` / `jwt_decode`). A PyPI token is `pypi-` + a base64-serialized **macaroon**, and the macaroon's first-party caveats are **JSON restrictions that remain readable without the issuing secret** — so a captured token reveals *how much* it can do, the question that drives supply-chain incident response: account-wide upload vs scoped to one project until next week. The new **`internal/macaroon`** package decodes both libmacaroons binary serializations (v1 packet format + v2 binary format, the format being rescrv/libmacaroons `doc/format.txt`); **`internal/pypitoken`** interprets the caveats — every documented form: the modern array tags (0=date window, 1=project-names, 2=project-IDs, 3=user-ID) and the three legacy object forms (noop / project-names / date) — and summarises the scope in one line (`account-wide` when nothing narrows it, otherwise the specific projects / window / user). **No confidently-wrong output**: it asserts **structure and scope only** — never that the token is live (needs a PyPI API call) and never verifying the macaroon signature (needs the issuing root key, which a captured token never carries); an unrecognised caveat is surfaced with its raw JSON, not guessed. **Wrap-vs-native: native** — `internal/macaroon` (stdlib `encoding/binary` varint) + `encoding/json`, **no new go.mod dep**; caveat tags/shapes taken from pypi/warehouse + the pypitoken library (`restrictions.py`). Verification is **doubly anchored to real output**: the binary parser is checked against the **pymacaroons cross-implementation v1+v2 vectors** (`serialization_tests.py`, generated by go-macaroon — identical fields from both), and the caveat decoder against **real pypitoken-generated tokens** covering all seven restriction kinds plus multi-caveat (scope `account-wide`, `projects: …`, date window, project-IDs, user-ID, legacy noop/projects/date). `secret_identify` routes `pypi-` to a full structural decode. Fuzz-clean (~6.8M execs, 0 panics); lint/vet/full-suite green. `internal/macaroon` + `internal/pypitoken`. Registry 638→639.
- `ir_raw_decode` + `ir_raw_encode` NEC42 / NEC42ext — ✅ v0.658.0 — rounded out the **NEC family** in the raw-IR decoder + encoder with **NEC42** (a 42-bit NEC variant carrying a **13-bit address + 8-bit command**, used by devices needing a larger address space than standard 8-bit NEC).
